Ruling on Selling Mixed Dirhams with Excess
States ruling and reasoning for permissibility of selling mixed dirhams with excess

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling mixed dirhams with excess, explain the qiyas reasoning, and identify the exception for high-value coins.

Core Idea
Mixed dirhams are treated as two commodities, allowing excess, but later scholars blocked riba by forbidding it for valuable coins.

You've learned the general rules of sarf: like-for-like exchange must be equal and hand-to-hand. But what if the coin contains a mixture of silver and alloy? The Hanafi school gives a nuanced ruling that balances the literal composition with the prevention of riba. This passage from al-Hidayah presents the general permissibility based on treating the mixture as two separate types, and then notes the precautionary fatwa for certain high-value coins. Let's work through the legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling mixed dirhams (containing silver and alloy) for their own kind with an excess. The general ruling is that it is permissible (ja'iz).
2
Understand the legal construct
The mixed dirham is treated as two separate commodities: silver and the alloy. By assigning each part to the opposite type in the exchange, the sale becomes like selling different types where excess is allowed.
This is a qiyas on the exchange of different types (e.g., silver for brass).
3
Recognize the sarf requirement
Despite the above, the contract remains sarf because silver is present on both sides. Therefore, hand-to-hand possession (qabd) in the session is required for both the silver and the alloy, since the alloy cannot be separated without difficulty.
The requirement of possession extends to the alloy as a necessary part of the coin.
4
Note the exception for high-value coins
The later shuyukh (scholars of Samarkand and Bukhara) did not give a fatwa on permissibility for 'adali and ghitarifah dirhams because they were high-value coins. Allowing excess would open the door to riba.
This is an application of sadd al-dhara'i (blocking the means to evil).
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: for ordinary mixed dirhams, the sale with excess is allowed (with possession condition); for 'adali and ghitarifah, it is not mufta bihi, so it is effectively prohibited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان بيعت بجنسها متفاضلا جاز صرفاً للجنس الى خلاف الجنس فهى فى حكم شيئين فضة وصفر ولكنه صرف حتى يشترط القبض فى المجلس لوجود الفضة من الجانبين فاذا شرط القبض في الفضة يشترط في الصفر لانه لا يتميز عنه الا بضرر قال رضى الله عنه ومشائخنا لم يفتوا بجواز ذلك في العدالى والغطارفة لانها اعز الاموال في ديارنا فلوا بيح التفاضل فيه يفتح باب الربوا
For if they (mixed dirhams) are sold for their own kind with excess, it is permissible, turning the type to a different type, so they are in the ruling of two things: silver and brass. But it is sarf such that hand-to-hand possession in the session is stipulated due to the presence of silver from both sides. So when possession is stipulated in silver, it is also stipulated in brass because it cannot be distinguished from it except with difficulty. He (the author) said, may Allah be pleased with him: And our shuyukh did not give a fatwa on the permissibility of that in 'adali and ghitarifah because they are the most precious wealth in our lands, so if excess is allowed in them, the door of riba would be opened.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Sarf deals with the ruling on selling mixed dirhams (dirhams that contain an alloy of copper or brass) for their own kind with an excess. The ruling is that such a sale is permissible, but with specific conditions. The reasoning is that mixed dirhams are treated as two distinct commodities: silver and the alloy. By assigning each part to the opposite type, the sale becomes like an exchange of different types where excess is allowed. However, despite this, the contract is still considered sarf (currency exchange) because silver is present on both sides. Therefore, the requirement of hand-to-hand possession in the session applies to both the silver and the alloy, since the alloy cannot be separated from the silver without difficulty. The author then notes that his shuyukh did not give a fatwa on permissibility for two specific types of coins: 'adali and ghitarifah. These were high-value coins in their region, and allowing excess would open the door to riba. This shows how the later scholars prioritized preventing harm over the abstract permissibility of the ruling.

Key Terms
دراهم مغشوشة (darahim mughshushah): These are coins that are mixed with other metals such as copper or brass, where the alloy (ghish) is present alongside silver.
عدالي ('adali): A type of dirham named after a king, 'Adali, characterized by a high content of alloy.
غطارفة (ghitarifah): Dirhams attributed to Ghitarif ibn 'Ata al-Kindi, an amir of Khurasan, also with predominant alloy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the later shuyukh not give a fatwa on permissibility of selling 'adali dirhams with excess?
A) Because 'adali dirhams have no silver content
B) To block the door to riba since they were high-value coins
C) Because the ruling only applies to gold coins
D) Because the shuyukh disagreed with the analogy

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, mixed dirhams are treated as two commodities for the purpose of riba, but the later scholars prioritized blocking the means to riba for high-value coins, showing how fiqh adapts to prevent harm.

Mixed Dirhams: Custom, Currency, and Partial Acceptance
Ruling on mixed dirhams: custom, currency status, and partial acceptance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on transactions with mixed dirhams, identify the governing custom (weight or count), classify them as currency or commodity, and apply the rule for partially accepted coins.

Core Idea
Mixed dirhams are fungible currency if current, become a commodity when not, and partial acceptance treats them like counterfeit coins.

This passage from Al-Hidayah lays out the Hanafi framework for mixed dirhams—coins that are not pure silver but are still used in trade. The ruling depends on three factors: market custom (whether they are traded by weight or count), whether they are still current as currency, and whether all parties accept them. Understanding these distinctions ensures valid contracts and prevents disputes.

Guided Path
1
Determine the Customary Mode
Identify whether the mixed dirhams are customarily transacted by weight or by count. The contract must follow that custom. If both customs exist, either is acceptable.
The custom at the time of contract is decisive.
2
Assess Currency Status
Determine if the dirhams are still current (accepted in the market). If yes, they are thaman (currency) and fungible—identification does not affect validity. If no longer current, they become sil'ah (commodity) and are specified by identification.
The status changes over time; treat each transaction by the status at that moment.
3
Apply Partial Acceptance Rule
If only some people accept the coins, they are analogous to zuyuf (counterfeit). The contract attaches to the type (zuyuf or jayyid) based on the seller's knowledge. If the seller knows the true nature, consent is presumed for zuyuf type; if ignorant, consent is presumed for good type.
Knowledge affects the validity of the contract: ignorance may lead to a defect (fasād) if the coins turn out to be bad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ان كانت تروج بالوزن فالتبايع والاستقراض فيها بالوزن وان كانت تروج بالعد فبالعد وان كانت تروج بهما فبكل واحد منهما لأن المعتبر هو المعتاد فيهما اذا لم يكن فيها نص ثم هى ما دامت تروج تكون اثمانا لا تتعين بالتعيين واذا كانت لا تروج فهى سلعة تتعين بالتعين واذا كانت يتقبلها البعض دون البعض فهى كالزيوف لا يتعلق العقد بعينها بل بجنسها زيوفا ان كان البائع يعلم بحالها لتحقق الرضاء منه وبجنسها من الجياد ان كان لا يعلم لعدم الرضاء منه
Then if they (mixed dirhams) are current by weight, then buying, selling, and borrowing in them is by weight. And if they are current by count, then by count. And if they are current by both, then by each one of them, because what is considered is the custom in them when there is no text. Then as long as they are current, they are thaman (currency) and are not specified by identification. And if they are not current, then they are a commodity (sil'ah) and become specified by identification. And if some people accept them and some do not, then they are like zuyuf (counterfeit coins); the contract does not attach to their specific entity but to their type as zuyuf if the seller knows their condition due to the realization of his consent, and to their type of good (jayyid) if he does not know due to lack of consent.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage provides detailed rules for mixed dirhams that are not pure silver but still used in transactions. The first principle is that the customary mode of transaction (weight or count) governs when there is no explicit nass (textual evidence). If people commonly trade by weight, then all transactions (sale, purchase, loan) must be by weight; if by count, then by count; if both, either is acceptable. The second principle concerns the legal status of these dirhams: as long as they are current and accepted in the market, they function as thaman (currency) and are fungible—they do not become specific by identification (like a specific coin). If they are no longer current, they become a commodity (sil'ah) and are then specific; identifying a particular coin has legal effect. The third principle deals with dirhams that are not universally accepted: some people accept them, others reject them. Such dirhams are analogous to counterfeit coins (zuyuf). The contract does not attach to the specific coin but to its type. If the seller knows the coins are of this mixed type, then the contract is valid based on his consent. If he does not know and thinks they are good coins, then the contract attaches to the type of good coins (jayyid), and the sale may be defective if they turn out to be mixed. This shows the importance of knowledge and consent in contracts.

Key Terms
ثمن (thaman): Currency or money; something used as a medium of exchange. Here, mixed dirhams that are still current are treated as thaman and are fungible.
سلعة (sil'ah): Commodity or good; when mixed dirhams are no longer current, they become a commodity and are specified by identification.
زيوف (zuyuf): Counterfeit or bad coins that are not universally accepted. Here, mixed dirhams that some reject are treated like zuyuf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When mixed dirhams are no longer current, what is their legal status?
A) They remain as thaman
B) They become a commodity (sil'ah)
C) They become like gold
D) They become invalid

Key Takeaway
The legal treatment of mixed dirhams hinges on market custom (weight/count) and whether they are current as currency; if partially accepted, the seller's knowledge determines the type of contract.

Rulings on Debased Coins: Custom, Currency, and Kind
Rulings on maghshush coins: custom, currency status, and partial acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three rulings on maghshush coins and apply the correct transaction method based on custom and circulation status.

Core Idea
Debased coins follow custom for weight/count; they act as currency if circulated, otherwise commodities; partial acceptance forces kind-based contracts.

The Hanafi school provides a nuanced framework for debased coins (maghshush), where base metals dominate. This passage from Al-Hidayah outlines three key rulings: the method of transaction depends on local custom; the legal status shifts from currency (thaman) to commodity (sila) based on circulation; and when coins are only partially accepted, the contract focuses on the kind, not the specific coin. Understanding these rules is crucial for avoiding riba in exchange and ensuring contracts are valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The passage presents three distinct situations: (a) determining weight vs count based on custom; (b) status while in circulation vs after demonetization; (c) partial acceptance by the public.
These scenarios are sequential and build on each other.
2
Apply the 'urf principle
Recognize that in the absence of explicit text (nass), local custom ('urf) determines the method of transaction (weight or count). This is a key Hanafi usul principle: 'al-'urf muhakkam' (custom is authoritative).
This applies to both buying/selling and lending.
3
Distinguish between thaman and sila
Coins in circulation are considered thaman (price) and are not specified by identification; if destroyed before delivery, the contract continues and the buyer must pay the equivalent. If not circulated, they become sila (commodities) and are specified; destruction voids the contract.
The critical factor is whether the coins are commonly accepted as money.
4
Analyze partial acceptance
When only some people accept the coins, they are treated like counterfeit (zuyuf). If the seller knows this, the contract binds the buyer to deliver counterfeit kind (since the seller is presumed to consent). If the seller does not know, the buyer must deliver good (full-bodied) coins, as consent to inferior coins is not established.
This scenario bridges the thaman/sila distinction.
5
Synthesize the framework
Combine the three rulings to determine the correct legal outcome for any given transaction involving maghshush coins: first check local custom for method, then circulation status for thaman/sila, and finally degree of acceptance for kind-based rulings.
Practice with multiple examples to internalize the conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصرف
ثم ان كانت تروج بالوزن فالتبايع والاستقراض فيها بالوزن وان كانت تروج بالعد فبالعد وان كانت تروج بهما فبكل واحد منهما لأن المعتبر هو المعتاد فيهما اذا لم يكن فيها نص ثم هى ما دامت تروج تكون اثمانا لا تتعين بالتعيين واذا كانت لا تروج فهى سلعة تتعين بالتعين واذا كانت يتقبلها البعض دون البعض فهى كالزيوف لا يتعلق العقد بعينها بل بجنسها زيوفا ان كان البائع يعلم بحالها لتحقق الرضاء منه وبجنسها من الجياد ان كان لا يعلم لعدم الرضاء منه
صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ وہ دراہم مغشوشہ جن میں پیتل غالب اور چاندی مغلوب ہواور وہ دنا نیر مغشوشہ جن میں سونا مغلوب اور پیتل وغیرہ کوئی دھات غالب ہو تو ان کا رواج اگر وزن کے ساتھ ہو تو ان کی خرید و فروخت کرنا اور قرض لینا دینا وزن کے حساب سے رہے گا اور اگر لوگوں میں ان کا رواج شمار اور گنتی سے ہو تو ان کی خرید و فروخت اور قرض کا لین دین شمار کر کے اور گنتی سے رہے گا۔ اور اگر ان کا رواج وزن اور گفتی دونوں سے ہو تو ان کی خرید و فروخت اور قرض کے لین دین میں وزن اور گفتی دونوں کا اعتبار کیا جائے ۔ گا یعنی وزن سے بھی ان کا لین دین کیا جا سکتا ہے اور گنتی سے بھی کیا جا سکتا ہے دلیل یہ ہے کہ ایسے دراہم مغشوشہ اور دنا نیر مغشوشہ میں لوگوں کی عادت ہی معتبر ہے اور ان میں لوگوں کی عادت اس لئے معتبر ہے کہ ایسے دراہم مغشوشہ اور دنا نیر مغشوشہ کے موزونی یا معدودی ہونے پر کوئی نص وارد نہیں ہوئی ہے۔ پس جب ان میں نص وارد نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے لوگوں کی عادت معتبر ہے تو وزن کرنے یا شمار کرنے کی جو بھی عادت ہوگی اس کا اعتبار کیا جائے گا۔
صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ ایسے دراہم مغشوشہ اور دنا نیر مغشوشہ جب تک رائج رہیں گے یعنی حکومت کی طرف سے جب تک شمن طور پر ان کا عام رواج رہے گا تو شریعت میں بھی ان کا شمار ثمن میں ہوتا رہے گا اور ثمن چونکہ متعین کرنے سے متعین نہیں ہوتا۔ اس لئے یہ بھی متعین کرنے سے متعین نہ ہوں گے چنانچہ عقد کرنے کے بعد اگر یہ در اہم مغشوشہ یا دنا نیر مغشوشہ بائع کو سپرد کرنے سے پہلے مشتری کے پاس سے ہلاک ہو گئے تو عقد باطل نہیں ہوگا بلکہ مشتری پر ان کے مثل دوسرے درا ہم واجب ہوں گے کیونکہ ثمن کے ہلاک ہونے سے عقد باطل نہیں ہوتا۔ اور اگر ایسے دراہم مغشوشہ کا حکومت کی طرف سے عام رواج نہ رہے تو ایسی صورت میں ان کوشن شمار نہیں کیا جائے گا بلکہ ان کو سامان شمار کیا جائے گا اور سامان چونکہ متعین کرنے سے متعین ہو جاتا ہے اس لئے یہ بھی متعین کرنے سے متعین ہو جائیں گے چنانچہ اگر کسی نے ایسے دراہم مغشوشہ یاد نانیہ مغشوشہ کوفروخت کیا اور پھر سپر د کر نے سے پہلے پہلے ہلاک ہو گئے تو عقد باطل ہو جائے گا جیسا کہ سپرد کرنے سے پہلے مبیع ہلاک ہونے سے عقد باطل ہو جاتا ہے اور اگر ایسے دراہم مغشوشہ اور دنا نیر مغشوشہ کو بعض لوگ قبول کرتے ہوں اور بعض قبول نہ کرتے ہیں سینہ تو یہ رائج ہی یہ کل یہ تھی اور کا سر ہیں تو ایسی صورت میں ان کا حکم زیوف یعنی کھوٹے دراہم اور دنا نیر کے مانند ہوگا۔ یعنی اگر بائع کو دراہم مغشوشہ اور دنا نیر مغشوشہ کا یہ حال معلوم ہے کہ ان کو بعض لوگ قبول کرتے ہیں اور بعض لوگ قبول نہیں کرتے تو عقد ان کی ذات سے متعلق نہ ہوگا بلکہ ان کی جنس یعنی کھوٹے دراہم یا کھوئے دنا نیر ہی واجب ہوں گے اور بائع کھوٹے ہی پائے گا۔ کیونکہ بائع کی طرف سے کھوٹوں پر رضا مندی ثابت ہو چکی ہے اور اگر بائع درا ہم مغشوشہ اور دنا نیر مغشوشہ کے حال سے واقف نہ ہو یعنی یہ معلوم نہ ہو کہ انکو بعض لوگ قبول کرتے ہیں اور بعض قبول نہیں کرتے تو ایسی صورت میں عقد گھرے اور جید دراہم کے ساتھ متعلق ہوگا اور مشتری پر کھرے دراہم کی ادائیگی واجب ہوگی کیونکہ اس صورت میں بائع کی طرف سے کھوئے دراہم پر رضامندی ظاہر نہیں ہوئی۔
Book of Sarf. Then if they (debased coins) are circulated by weight, then buying, selling, and borrowing in them is by weight. If they are circulated by count, then by count. If they are circulated by both, then by each of them, because what is considered is the customary practice in them when there is no text (nass) regarding them. Then, as long as they are in circulation, they are considered prices (athman), not specified by specification. When they are not in circulation, they are commodities (sila'), specified by specification. And if some people accept them and others do not, then they are like counterfeit coins (zuyuf); the contract is not attached to their specific identity but to their kind (jins) as counterfeit, if the seller knows their condition, due to his satisfaction being realized; and to their kind as good coins if he does not know, due to lack of satisfaction.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage from Al-Hidayah, explained by the commentator, presents the rulings for maghshush (debased) coins—dirhams and dinars where the base metal predominates. The first ruling concerns the method of transaction: If such coins are customarily traded by weight, then sales and loans must be conducted by weight; if by count, then by count; if both, then either is permissible. The basis is local custom ('urf) since no divine text specifies the method. The second ruling addresses their legal status: While they remain in common circulation as currency (thaman), they are like full-bodied money—they do not become specified by individual identification. Thus, if they are destroyed before delivery, the contract remains valid and the buyer must pay their equivalent. If they cease to circulate and become mere commodities, they are specified by identification; destruction before delivery invalidates the contract, like any goods. The third ruling deals with partial acceptance: If some people accept them and others do not, they are treated like counterfeit coins (zuyuf). If the seller knows this, the contract binds the buyer to deliver counterfeit coins (the kind agreed upon), as consent is assumed. If the seller is unaware, the buyer must deliver good (full-bodied) coins because the seller did not consent to inferior coins. The commentator emphasizes that these rulings are based on custom and the absence of explicit textual evidence, and they are applied by Hanafi scholars to prevent usury (riba) in currency exchange.

Key Terms
مغشوش (maghshūsh): Debased coin where the base metal (e.g., brass) is dominant and the precious metal (silver or gold) is subordinate.
ثمن (thaman): Price or currency; when coins are in circulation, they function as thaman and are not specified by identity.
سلعة (sila' (or sil'a)): Commodity; when coins are no longer circulated, they become like goods and are specified by identification.
عرف (urf): Customary practice; the basis for determining method of transaction (weight vs count) when no text exists.
زيوف (zuyūf): Counterfeit or very debased coins that are not generally accepted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if maghshush dirhams that are not in circulation (no longer used as money) are destroyed before delivery to the seller?
A) The contract remains valid and the buyer must deliver similar dirhams.
B) The contract becomes void (batil).
C) The buyer must deliver good (full-bodied) dirhams.
D) The buyer is excused from payment.

Key Takeaway
Debased coins shift from non-specified currency (thaman) to specified commodity (sila) based on circulation, and partial acceptance is governed by the seller's knowledge.

8 min
Mixed Coins: Custom Determines Their Legal Status in Sales and Loans
Explains the legal treatment of mixed coins based on custom and circulation status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the legal treatment of mixed coins (maghshushah) depends on custom ('urf) and circulation status, distinguishing between their roles as thaman (price) and sama' (goods).

Core Idea
The treatment of mixed coins depends on custom – not text – and their status changes when they lose currency.

This passage from Al-Hidayah deals with darahim maghshushah (mixed dirhams) and dananir maghshushah (mixed dinars) – coins where the base metal dominates the precious metal. Learn how their legal classification as price (thaman) or commodity (sama') depends on custom and whether they remain current.

Guided Path
1
Define mixed coins
Identify the composition: base metal (e.g., brass) dominant, precious metal (silver or gold) subdued. This contrasts with pure coins.
The Hanafi definition is critical; the ruling does not apply to pure coins.
2
Determine the method of circulation
Check whether the coins are transacted by weight, count, or both. The custom (urf) of the people governs which method must be used in sales and loans.
If custom is by count, weight is not sufficient; if by both, either is valid.
3
Analyze the proof (dalil)
The passage states that no revealed text (nass) specifies a method for these coins, so custom is authoritative. This is a key Hanafi principle: where nass is silent, 'urf is binding.
This reasoning is based on the absence of evidence, not a positive text.
4
Distinguish current vs. obsolete coins
While coins remain current (ra'ij), they are considered thaman (price) and are non-specific (ghayr mu'ayyan). If destroyed before delivery, the buyer must replace with similar coins. When circulation ceases, they become sama' (goods) and are specific; destruction voids the contract.
The shift from thaman to sama' is triggered by demonetization by the government.
5
Apply the rule for partial acceptance
If some people accept the mixed coins and others do not, they are treated like counterfeit coins (zuyuf). The seller's knowledge determines liability: if the seller knows, the debt is in impure coins; if unaware, in pure coins.
This rule protects the seller's consent, which is not given to coins of uncertain acceptability.

And the contract will be related to the species of pure dirhams, provided he does not know them (to be mixed). Because consent to impure dirhams is not found from his side. Explanation: The author of Hidayah says: Those mixed dirhams in which brass is dominant and silver is subdued, and those mixed dinars in which gold is subdued and brass or some other metal is dominant, then if their circulation is by weight, then their buying, selling, and lending will be by weight; and if their circulation among people is by count, then their buying, selling, and lending will be by count; and if their circulation is by both weight and count, then in their buying, selling, and lending both weight and count will be considered. That is, transactions can be done with them both by weight and by count. The proof is that in such mixed dirhams and mixed dinars, the custom of people is authoritative, and their custom is authoritative because there is no text (nass) regarding these mixed dirhams and mixed dinars being by weight or by count. So when, due to the absence of text, people's custom is authoritative, then whatever custom exists regarding weighing or counting will be considered. The author of Hidayah says: As long as such mixed dirhams and mixed dinars remain current, i.e., as long as they have general circulation as silver from the government, they will be considered as silver in Shariah, and since silver is not determined by specification, these also will not be determined by specification. Thus, after the contract, if these mixed dirhams or mixed dinars are destroyed with the buyer before delivery to the seller, the contract does not become void; rather, the buyer must pay similar other dirhams, because the contract does not become void by the destruction of the price. And if such mixed dirhams no longer have general circulation from the government, then in that case they will not be considered as silver; rather, they will be considered as goods, and since goods become determined by specification, these also will become determined by specification. Thus, if someone sold such mixed dirhams or mixed dinars and then they were destroyed before delivery, the contract becomes void, just as the contract becomes void when the sold item (mabee') is destroyed before delivery. And if some people accept such mixed dirhams and mixed dinars and some do not accept them, then in that case their ruling is like zuyuf (counterfeit dirhams and dinars). That is, if the seller knows the condition of the mixed dirhams and mixed dinars – that some people accept them and some do not – then the contract will not relate to their specific identity; rather, their species, i.e., impure dirhams or impure dinars, will be obligatory, and the seller will receive impure ones. Because consent to impure ones is proven from the seller. And if the seller is not aware of the condition of the mixed dirhams and mixed dinars – i.e., he does not know that some people accept them and some do not – then in that case the contract will relate to pure and genuine dirhams, and the buyer must pay pure dirhams, because in this case consent to impure dirhams is not apparent from the seller.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary (sharh) on a ruling from Al-Hidayah regarding mixed coins (maghshushah) in sales and loans. The author first defines maghshushah dirhams and dinars as those where the base metal is dominant and the precious metal is subdued. The ruling is that their treatment depends on the custom ('urf) of the people: if they circulate by weight, transactions are by weight; if by count, by count; if both, then both are permissible. The proof for this is the absence of a revealed text (nass) specifying a single method, so custom governs. Next, the passage distinguishes between coins that remain current (ra'ij) and those that become obsolete. While current, they function as thaman (price) and are not specific; if destroyed before delivery, the buyer must replace them with similar coins. When circulation ceases (government no longer recognizes them), they become like goods (sama') and are specific; if destroyed before delivery, the contract becomes void, similar to the destruction of the sold item. Finally, the case where some people accept them and some do not is analogous to counterfeit coins (zuyuf): if the seller knows this, the debt is in the species of impure coins; if the seller does not know, the debt is in pure coins because his consent is only to pure coins.

Key Terms
دراہم مغشوشة (darahim maghshushah): Coins where the base metal (e.g., brass) is dominant and silver is minor.
ثمن (thaman): Price or medium of exchange; here, refers to the status of being a legal currency.
سامان (sama' (or sāmān)): Goods or commodity; when coins become obsolete, they are treated as fungible goods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If mixed dirhams (maghshushah) are current by custom of counting, and the buyer pays by weight, what is the ruling?
A) The transaction is valid only if done by count.
B) The transaction is valid by weight as well, since weight is also acceptable.
C) The transaction is void because the custom is not followed.
D) The transaction is valid only if both parties agree to weight.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school relies on custom ('urf) to determine the method of transacting mixed coins, and their status as price or goods changes with circulation.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Sale with Obsolete Currency
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sale with obsolete currency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the differences between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding a sale when the currency becomes obsolete, and apply the correct ruling for each valuation date.

Core Idea
When currency becomes obsolete, Abu Hanifa voids the sale; the Sahibayn preserve it but differ on valuation date.

This passage addresses a critical intra-Hanafi dispute: what happens to a sale when the coins used as payment become obsolete before delivery? Abu Hanifa argues the price loses its legal status, voiding the contract, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad maintain the contract remains valid but require payment of value. Understanding this dispute illuminates how Hanafi scholars balance contractual validity with the reality of currency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and central question
A sale is made using mixed coins (maghshushah) that later become obsolete (kasād). The question: does the sale remain valid or become void?
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa and his dalil
Abu Hanifa declares the sale void because the price (thaman) loses its legal status by convention (istilah); without valid price, the contract fails.
His proof: thamanīya is established by convention, not inherent.
3
State the position of the Sahibayn and their dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold the sale valid despite obsolescence; impossibility of specific delivery does not invalidate the contract, analogized to fresh dates ceasing to be available.
4
Analyze the secondary dispute on valuation date
Abu Yusuf values the coins on the contract day, Muhammad on the last day of dealing. This affects the amount owed.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Abu Hanifa: return goods or pay value if destroyed. Under Sahibayn: buyer pays qīmah (value) of coins, with differing dates.

And if he buys goods with them (the mixed coins), then they become obsolete and people abandon dealing with them, then according to Abu Hanifa the sale is void. Abu Yusuf said: upon him is their value on the day of the sale. Muhammad said: their value on the last day people dealt with them. Their (the Sahibayn) proof is that the contract was valid, but delivery became impossible due to obsolescence, and that does not necessitate invalidity, just as if one buys with fresh dates and they cease. And if the contract remains, the value is obligatory, but according to Abu Yusuf at the time of the sale because it is guaranteed by it, and according to Muhammad on the day of cessation because that is when transition to value occurs. And Abu Hanifa's proof is that the price (thaman) is destroyed by obsolescence, because its being price (thaman) was by convention, and since that no longer remains, the sale remains without price, hence void. And when the sale is void, the sold item must be returned if it still exists, or its value if it is destroyed, as in a void sale (fasad). Translation: And if someone buys goods in exchange for such mixed dirhams, then their circulation stops and people stop dealing with them, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa the sale becomes void. Imam Abu Yusuf said: upon the buyer is the value of those mixed dirhams on the day of the contract. Imam Muhammad said: their value on the last day people dealt with them. The proof of the Sahibayn is that the contract was valid, but delivery became impossible due to obsolescence, and that does not necessitate invalidity, just as if someone buys something in exchange for fresh dates and then (the dates) disappear from the market. And when the contract remains, the value is obligatory; but according to Abu Yusuf it is the value at the time of the sale because the price is guaranteed by it, and according to Muhammad it is the value on the day of cessation because that is the time of transition to value. And the proof of Abu Hanifa is that the price is destroyed by obsolescence, because its being price was by convention, and since that convention no longer remains, the sale remains without price, so it becomes void. And when the sale becomes void, the sold item must be returned if it exists, or its value if it is destroyed, as in a void sale. Explanation: The scenario is that if someone buys goods in exchange for such mixed dirhams in which impurity is dominant, then those dirhams become obsolete, i.e., their circulation stops and people stop dealing with them, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa this sale becomes void. Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad said that the sale does not become void; rather, the buyer must pay their value. Imam Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad are also of this opinion. As for which day's value is obligatory, the Sahibayn also differ: Imam Abu Yusuf said that on the day the sale contract took place, the value of those mixed dirhams is obligatory upon the buyer; and Imam Muhammad said that on the last day people dealt with them, whatever their value was, that is obligatory. The Sahibayn's proof is that because the exchange of property for property took place, the delivery of the price became impossible, and if delivery becomes impossible, that does not cause the contract to become void, just as if someone buys something in exchange for fresh dates and then before delivery the dates cease to be available.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf) regarding a sale where the currency (mixed coins) becomes obsolete after the contract but before delivery. The issue is whether the sale remains valid or becomes void, and if valid, what value must be paid. Abu Hanifa holds that the sale becomes void because the price (thaman) loses its status as legal tender by obsolescence, so the contract is without a valid price. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider the contract valid because it was formed correctly; the subsequent impossibility of delivering that specific price does not invalidate it, analogous to buying with fresh dates that then become unavailable. However, they differ on the valuation date: Abu Yusuf takes the contract date, Muhammad takes the date of obsolescence. The commentary adds that Imam Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad agree with the Sahibayn on the validity of the sale. The passage concludes by noting that if the sale is void (per Abu Hanifa), the buyer must return the goods if extant, or pay its value if destroyed, as in a void sale (bay' fasid).

Key Terms
كساد (kasād): Obsolescence or depression of coins; they cease to circulate as currency.
ثمنية (thamanīya): The quality of being a price (thaman); its legal status as currency.
قيمة (qīmah): Monetary value or worth; to be paid when the specific thaman cannot be delivered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Yusuf, if mixed dirhams become obsolete after a sale, what must the buyer pay?
A) The value of the sold goods on the contract day.
B) The value of the mixed dirhams on the contract day.
C) The value of the mixed dirhams on the day they became obsolete.
D) The same mixed dirhams if he can find them.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a sale with obsolete currency depends on whether the price's existence is by convention or delivery possibility; Hanafi masters differ accordingly.

Sale with Obsolete Dirhams: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Analyzing the ruling when dirhams become obsolete after purchase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad regarding the sale with obsolete dirhams and explain the legal reasoning behind each position.

Core Idea
Custom (urf) can determine the nature of currency; when custom collapses, the price may perish, voiding the sale—but the Sahibayn disagree.

This lesson examines a classical intra-Hanafi dispute about the fate of a sale when the currency used as price becomes obsolete after the contract. The disagreement turns on whether the price (thaman) is an intrinsic or conventional entity. You will learn the arguments of the three early Hanafi masters and the practical consequences of their rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Read the case: a buyer purchases a commodity with mixed dirhams (maghshūsha), then those dirhams become obsolete (kāsida) before delivery.
The dirhams are not pure silver; their value depends on acceptance in transactions.
2
List the Three Positions
Abu Hanifa: the sale becomes void (bātil). Abu Yusuf: the sale is valid; the buyer owes the value of the dirhams at the time of sale. Muhammad: the sale is valid; the buyer owes the value at the last day of circulation.
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) agree on validity but differ on valuation date.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's Proof
They argue that a valid contract is not corrupted by subsequent impossibility of delivery, analogizing to a purchase with fresh dates that become unavailable. The price converts to an obligation of value.
Their qiyās treats the price as a fungible that can be compensated by value.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's Proof
Abu Hanifa argues that the price's status is purely conventional (bi al-istilāh). When people stop accepting the dirhams, the price perishes, leaving a sale without a price, which is void.
He distinguishes between price and commodity: the price itself ceases to exist as a legal entity.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilāf)
Under Abu Hanifa, the buyer returns the commodity or its value (if destroyed) to the seller. Under the Sahibayn, the buyer pays the value of the dirhams at the specified date, and keeps the commodity.
The difference affects which party bears the loss from the obsolescence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا اشترى بها سلعة فكسدت وترك الناس المعاملة بها بطل البيع عند أبي حنيفة وقال أبو يوسف عليه قيمتها يوم البيع وقال محمد قيمتها آخر ما تعامل الناس بها لهما أن العقد قد صح إلا أنه تعذر التسليم بالكساد وأنه لا يوجب الفساد كما إذا اشترى بالرطب فانقطع وإذا بقي العقد وجبت القيمة لكن عند أبي يوسف وقت البيع لأنه مضمون به وعند محمد يوم الانقطاع لأنه أول الانتقال إلى القيمة ولأبي حنيفة أن الثمن يهلك بالكساد لأن الثمنية بالاصطلاح وما بقي فيبقى بيعا بلا ثمن فيبطل وإذا بطل البيع يجب رد المبيع إن كان قائما وقيمته إن كان هالكا كما في البيع الفاسد
And if one purchases a commodity with them (mixed dirhams) and they become obsolete and people cease transacting with them, the sale is void according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf said: upon him (the buyer) is their value on the day of the sale. Muhammad said: their value on the last day people transacted with them. Their (the Sahibayn's) proof: the contract was valid, but delivery became impossible due to obsolescence, and that does not necessitate corruption, just as if one purchases with fresh dates and they become unavailable. And if the contract remains, the value is obligatory, but according to Abu Yusuf at the time of sale because it is guaranteed thereby, and according to Muhammad on the day of unavailability because that is the first time of transition to value. And for Abu Hanifa: the price perishes by obsolescence because being a price is by convention, and what remains becomes a sale without a price, so it becomes void. And when the sale is void, returning the commodity is obligatory if it still exists, and its value if it is destroyed, as in a void sale.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of the obsolescence (kasād) of mixed dirhams (maghshūsha) used as the price in a sale after the contract but before delivery. The core disagreement: Does the price (thaman) perish when it becomes obsolete, voiding the sale, or does the sale remain valid with the price converting to a monetary obligation? Abu Hanifa argues that the price perishes because its status as thaman depends on conventional acceptance (istilāh). Once people stop transacting with it, the sale lacks a price, thus becomes void (bātil). Consequently, the buyer must return the commodity if it still exists or pay its value if destroyed, akin to a void sale. In contrast, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) argue that the contract was initially valid, and subsequent inability to deliver does not corrupt it. They analogize to buying with fresh dates (rutab) that become unavailable – the contract stands, and the buyer owes the value. However, they differ on valuation date: Abu Yusuf takes the day of sale (because the price was guaranteed from that moment), while Muhammad takes the last day of circulation (because that is when the obligation to pay value arises). Later scholars note that Imam al-Shafi‘i and Imam Ahmad agree with the Sahibayn. The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is not explicitly stated here, but in practice many fatāwā follow the Sahibayn. The passage exemplifies the Hanafi use of qiyās and the importance of custom ('urf) in determining the nature of currency.

Key Terms
مغشوشة (maghshūsha): Dirhams that are mixed with a base metal, having a majority of alloy, so their value is not intrinsic silver.
كساد (kasād): The condition where a currency ceases to be accepted in transactions, becoming obsolete.
ثمن (thaman): The price or medium of exchange in a sale.
اصطلاح (istilāh): Conventional agreement of the people, which determines the status of something as currency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what happens to a sale when the mixed dirhams used as the price become obsolete (kāsida) before delivery?
A) The sale remains valid and the buyer must pay the value at the time of sale.
B) The sale becomes void (bātil) and the buyer must return the commodity or pay its value.
C) The sale is suspended until the dirhams regain currency.
D) The seller must accept alternative payment.

Key Takeaway
When the price depends on custom, its obsolescence can void the sale per Abu Hanifa, but the Sahibayn prioritize contract validity and convert to a value obligation.

Ruling on Sale with Fulūs (Copper Coins)
Ruling on fulūs as price with condition-based specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using fulūs as price in a sale and distinguish between the conditions of nāfiqa and kāsida.

Core Idea
Fulūs are price by custom; when current, no specification; when obsolete, they are commodities.

This lesson examines a concise ruling from the Hanafi matn on the permissibility of using fulūs (copper coins) as the price in a sale. The core distinction is whether the coins are currently circulating (nāfiqa) or obsolete (kāsida). Understanding this distinction helps you apply the Hanafi principle that custom ('urf) can determine the legal nature of currency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its general ruling
The act is selling with fulūs as the price. The general ruling is that it is permissible (jā'iz) because fulūs are known property (māl ma'lūm).
This is the baseline ruling.
2
Assess the condition of the fulūs
Determine whether the fulūs are nāfiqa (currently accepted in transactions) or kāsida (obsolete, no longer commonly used).
3
Apply the rule based on condition
If nāfiqa, no specification (ta'yīn) is required; they are treated as prices by convention (athmān bil-istilāh). If kāsida, specification is required because they are considered commodities (sil'a).
The sale is not permissible (lā yajūz) without specification when kāsida.
4
Appreciate the rationale (talīl)
Fulūs derive their status as price from custom ('urf). When custom changes and they become obsolete, their legal nature reverts to that of a commodity.
This reflects the Hanafi reliance on 'urf as a source of law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ويجوز البيع بالفلوس لأنه مال معلوم فان كانت نافقة جاز البيع بها وان لم تعيين لأنها أثمان بالاصطلاح وان كانت كاسدة لم يجز البيع بها حتى يعينها لأنها سلع فلا بد من تعيينها
He said: And selling with fulūs (copper coins) is permissible because they are known property. So if they are current (nāfiqa), selling with them is permissible even if they are not specified, because they are prices by convention. And if they are obsolete (kāsida), selling with them is not permissible unless they are specified, because they are commodities, so their specification is necessary.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a concise ruling (ḥukm) on the permissibility of using fulūs (copper coins not of pure silver/gold) as the price (thaman) in a sale. The ruling: Fulūs are considered known property (māl ma'lūm). If they are currently circulating as a medium of exchange (nāfiqa), they function as price by custom (istilāh) and need not be individually specified in the contract—just like silver dirhams. However, if they have become obsolete (kāsida), they lose their status as price and revert to being a commodity (sil'a). In that case, they must be specifically identified (ta'yīn) in the sale contract, just like any other goods. The rationale: The value of fulūs as currency is purely conventional; when the convention ends, they are mere commodities. This ruling reflects the Hanafi reliance on custom ('urf) to determine the nature of currency. The text uses the matn form, likely from al-Qudūrī or a similar source, and is a standard memorization point in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
فلوس (fulūs): Copper or base metal coins used as currency by custom, not intrinsically valuable like gold or silver.
نافقة (nāfiqa): Current and commonly accepted in transactions.
كاسدة (kāsida): Obsolete, no longer commonly accepted as currency.
أثمان بالاصطلاح (athmān bil-istilāh): Prices by convention; not intrinsically price but accepted as such by mutual agreement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, always.
B) Yes, only if the amount is large.
C) No, because they are considered prices by convention.
D) No, but only if the seller agrees.

Key Takeaway
Fulūs are price by custom; if current, no specification; if obsolete, they require specification like any commodity.

Sale with Dirham Maghshush that Becomes Obsolete: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability when mixed dirhams become obsolete after sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three intra-Hanafi positions on liability when the price currency becomes obsolete after a sale, and derive the practical difference in compensation.

Core Idea
When the price currency loses thaman status, the three Hanafi imams differ on whether the sale is void or how to value the debt.

This passage examines a subtle scenario: a buyer pays with mixed dirhams (dirham maghshush) which later go out of circulation (kasād) before delivery. The three founding imams of the Hanafi school—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad—disagree on the legal outcome. Understanding this dispute sharpens your appreciation of how thaman (monetary value) and contractual liability interact in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Case and Variable
The case is a sale where the price consists of mixed dirhams that later become obsolete (kasād) after the contract but before delivery. The key variable is the legal effect of kasād on the validity of the sale and the buyer's obligation.
2
List Each Imam's Ruling and Reasoning
Abu Hanifa holds the sale void (bātil) because the coin ceased to be thaman by people's agreement. Abu Yusuf holds the sale valid but the buyer pays the value on the day of sale, analogizing to ghasb. Muhammad holds the sale valid but the buyer pays the value on the day of kasād, because the obligation to pay value arises only when the coin becomes obsolete.
Pay attention to how each imam defines the moment of liability (ḍamān).
3
Note the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: Under Abu Hanifa, the buyer must return the goods if they exist or pay their value/mithl. Under Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the sale stands and the buyer pays a monetary amount, but the valuation date differs—either the day of sale or the day of kasād.
This difference affects the amount owed if the value of the dirhams changed between the sale and obsolescence.

شئی مغصوب اسی دن میں مضمون ہوئی ہے اور امام محمد کے نزدیک جس دن در اہم مغشوشہ کا چین بازار سے بند ہوا ہے اس دن کی قیمت واجب ہوگی کیونکہ دراہم مغشوشہ سے منتقل ہو کر قیمت کی طرف آنا اسی دن واجب ہوا ہے تھا لیکن جب لوگوں نے ان کے ساتھ معاملہ کرنا چھوڑ دیا تو لوگوں کی اصطلاح اور اتفاق باطل ہو گیا اور جب لوگوں کا اتفاق باقی نہ رہا تو عقد مع بلاشن رہ گیا اور بلاشن عقد چونکہ باطل ہے اس لئے اس صورت میں متحقق ہو گا جب تمام شہروں میں چلن بند ہو جائے اور شیخین کے نزدیک کساد تحقق ہونے کے لئے اتنا کافی ہے کہ جس شہر میں عقد واقع ہوا ہے اسی شہر میں چلن بند ہو جائے ۔ ( مینی ) جمیل احمد
If dates become unavailable in the market, i.e., their availability ceases, then this sale does not become void by agreement, rather the buyer owes their price. And according to Badr al-Din al-Ayni, one will not wait for fresh dates next year. Thus here too the sale will not become void, and since it did not become void due to kasād (stagnation), rather the goods remain, the buyer owes the price of those mixed dirhams. But according to Imam Abu Yusuf, the price on the day of sale is owed because the liability for mixed dirhams arises from the sale in the same way. Thus if there were no sale, the buyer would not owe liability for those mixed dirhams. So since liability comes upon the buyer due to this sale, the price on the day of sale is considered. That is, the price of mixed dirhams on the day of sale is owed by the buyer, just as after the destruction of a usurped item, the usurper owes the price on the day of usurpation because the usurped thing became liable on that day. And according to Imam Muhammad, the price on the day when the mixed dirhams ceased to circulate is owed, because the transfer from mixed dirhams to price becomes obligatory on that day. So since the transfer to price occurred on that day, the price of that day is considered. The proof of Imam Abu Hanifa is: such mixed dirhams in which copper predominates became thaman by the agreement of people. But when people stopped dealing with them, their convention and agreement became void. And when their agreement no longer exists, the contract remains without thaman, and a contract without thaman is void. Therefore in this case also the contract becomes void. When the contract is void, the buyer must return the goods if they still exist. If the goods have perished, then their price is owed if the goods are from items of value (dhawāt al-qiyam). If the goods are from items of similars (dhawāt al-amthāl), then a similar item must be returned, as in the case of a void sale (bay' fasid). Benefit: Kasād means cessation of circulation. According to Imam Muhammad, kasād occurs when circulation stops in all cities. According to the two shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?), it suffices that circulation stops in the city where the contract took place. (Minī) Jamil Ahmad.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf) regarding a sale where the specified coin (dirham maghshush, i.e., mixed silver-copper coin) becomes demonetized (kasād) after the contract but before delivery or payment. The commentator explains the positions of the three imams. Imam Abu Hanifa rules that the sale becomes void (bātil) because the coin ceases to be thaman (currency) by people's agreement, so the contract lacks a valid price. Consequently, if the sold item (mabī') still exists, the buyer must return it; if it perished, the buyer must pay its value (if it is qimī) or a similar item (if it is mithlī). Imam Abu Yusuf holds that the sale remains valid but the buyer must pay the value of the coin on the day of the sale, analogizing to the case of a usurper (ghāṣib) who must pay value on the day of usurpation. Imam Muhammad holds that the buyer must pay the value on the day the coin ceased to circulate, because the obligation to pay value arises only at that moment. The passage also clarifies that 'kasād' (stagnation) is defined differently: according to Imam Muhammad, it means cessation in all cities; according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, or 'Shaykhayn' here referring to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad? The text says 'Shaykhayn' but context suggests the first two: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, but the earlier statement attributed to 'Shaykhayn' likely refers to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad as opposed to Abu Hanifa. However, the Urdu commentary states 'Imam Muhammad ke nazdik... aur Shaykhayn ke nazdik...' which indicates Shaykhayn are Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. This shows a difference in the scope of kasād.

Key Terms
كساد (kasād): Cessation of circulation of a coin as currency; demonetization.
ثمن (thaman): Price; the monetary countervalue in a sale.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability; the obligation to compensate for loss or damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what happens to a sale when the mixed dirham used as price becomes obsolete before delivery?
A) The sale remains valid and the buyer pays the value on the day of sale.
B) The sale becomes void and the buyer must return the sold item if it exists.
C) The sale remains valid and the buyer pays the value on the day of kasād.
D) The sale is suspended until the coin is replaced.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has three distinct views on sales with obsolete currency: Abu Hanifa voids the sale, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad preserve it but differ on valuation date.

Sale with Fulūs: Current vs. Obsolete Coins
Statement of permissibility and condition for fulūs sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sale with fulūs and identify when specification of the coins is required.

Core Idea
Fulūs are thaman by convention; only obsolete ones must be specified in sale.

Fulūs (copper coins) are not intrinsic currency like gold and silver, but become money by custom. This lesson explains the key distinction between current (nāfiqa) and obsolete (kāsida) fulūs. The ruling hinges on whether they function as price or commodity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Note that the author begins by stating that sale with fulūs is permissible. This is the baseline ruling.
2
Distinguish the two cases
The passage separates fulūs into current (nāfiqa) and obsolete (kāsida). For each, a different rule applies.
3
Apply the rule to current fulūs
If fulūs are in circulation, they are treated as thaman (price) by convention. Like dirhams, they need not be specified in the contract.
Specification is not only unnecessary but impossible because thaman is fungible.
4
Apply the rule to obsolete fulūs
If fulūs are no longer in circulation, they revert to being commodities (sila'). Therefore, they must be specified just like any other good.
Without specification, the sale is invalid due to gharar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ويجوز البيع بالفلوس لانه مال معلوم فان كانت نافقة جاز البيع بها وان لم تعيين لانها اثمان بالاصطلاح وان كانت كاسدة لم يجز البيع بها حتى يعينها لا نها سلع فلا بد من تعيينها

تشريح فلوس فلس کی جمع ہے بمعنی پیسہ، فلوس، چاندی، ہونے کے علاوہ دوسری دھاتوں تانے پینیل وغیرہ کے ہوتے ہیں۔ فلوں اپنی ذات کے اعتبار سے تو شمن نہیں ہوتے البتہ لوگوں کے اتفاق کرنے سے شمن ہو جاتے ہیں۔ یہ ہے کہ فلوس کے عوض بیع جائز ہے کیونکہ فلوس ایسا مال نہیں جو مقدار اور وصف کے اعتبار سے معلوم ہو جاتے ہیں اور جو مال معلوم المقدار اور معلوم الوصف ہو اس کے عوض بیج جائز ہوتی ہے اس لئے فلوس کے عوض بیع جائز ہو جائے گی اب وہ فلوس عقد کے وقت یا تو رائج ہوں گے یا ان کا رواج ٹھپ ہو چکا ہوگا۔ اگر وہ رائج ہیں تو دراہم ودنا نیر کی طرح بیچ میں ان کا متعین کرنا ضروری نہیں ہے بلکہ وہ متعین کرنے سے بھی متعین نہ ہوں گے مثلاً : دس پیسے کی کوئی چیز خریدی تو اس چیز کا معین کرنا واجب ہے مگر پیسے معین نہ ہوں گے بلکہ پیسوں میں دس پیسے دیدے کیونکہ لوگوں کے اتفاق کر لینے سے فلوس شمن ہو گئے ہیں اور شمن متعین کرنے سے متعین نہیں ہوتا اس لئے یہ فلوس بھی متعین نہ ہوں گے اور اگر وہ فلوس رائج نہ ہوں بلکہ ان کا چلن بند ہو چکا ہو تو بیع میں ان کا معین کرنا ضروری ہے یعنی اس صورت میں ان کی بیچ اس وقت جائز ہوگی جبکہ ان کو متعین کر دیا گیا ہو کیونکہ اب یہ فلوس، سامان ہیں اور سامان کی بیع میں سامان کو متعین کرنا ضروری ہوتا ہے اس لیے ان کو متعین کرنا بھی ضروری ہوگا۔
He said: Sale with fulūs is permissible because they are known property. If they are current (nāfiqa), sale with them is permissible even if they are not specified, because they are prices by convention (bi'l-iṣṭilāḥ). And if they are obsolete (kāsida), sale with them is not permissible unless they are specified, because they are commodities (sila'), so they must be specified. Commentary: Fulūs is the plural of fals, meaning coins. Fulūs are made of metals other than silver, such as copper, brass, etc. In themselves, fulūs are not thaman, but they become thaman by people's agreement. This is that sale in exchange for fulūs is permissible because fulūs are property that is known in quantity and description. And sale in exchange for property known in quantity and description is permissible. Thus sale in exchange for fulūs becomes permissible. Now those fulūs at the time of contract are either current or they have ceased to circulate. If they are current, then like dirhams and dinars, they need not be specified in the sale; rather, even if specified, they do not become specified. For example, if one buys something for ten coins, specifying the item is obligatory, but the coins are not specified; rather one gives ten coins, because fulūs become thaman by people's agreement, and thaman is not specified by specification. Thus these fulūs will not be specified. And if the fulūs are not current but have ceased to circulate, then their specification in the sale is necessary; that is, their sale is only permissible when they have been specified, because now these fulūs are commodities, and in the sale of a commodity, it is necessary to specify it. Therefore specifying them is also necessary.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a plain ruling statement (matn) from the author stating the basic rule for using fulūs (copper coins) as a price. The core principle: fulūs are not intrinsic currency like gold and silver but become thaman (price) by customary agreement (iṣṭilāḥ). When fulūs are current (nāfiqa), they function as currency and need not be specified in the contract; one simply pays the amount. This is analogous to dirhams and dinars. When fulūs are obsolete (kāsida), they revert to being mere commodities (sila') and must be specifically identified just like any other good in a sale. The Urdu commentary reinforces this, explaining that specification of current fulūs is not only unnecessary but impossible, because as thaman they are fungible. For obsolete fulūs, specification is a condition for validity.

Key Terms
فلوس (fulūs): Copper or base-metal coins used as currency by convention, not intrinsic value.
نافقة (nāfiqa): Current; in circulation as accepted currency.
كسادة (kāsida): Obsolete; no longer in circulation as currency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, when selling with fulūs that are current (nāfiqa), is it necessary to specify the exact coins?
A) Yes, because fulūs are commodities and must be specified.
B) No, because they are thaman by convention and do not need specification.
C) Only if the contract involves more than ten fulūs.
D) Only if the buyer requests specification.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes current fulūs (thaman, no specification needed) from obsolete fulūs (commodity, specification required).

The Dispute on Fulūs Becoming Obsolete Before Delivery
Intra-Hanafi dispute on effect of fulūs becoming obsolete after contract before delivery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on the effect of fulūs becoming obsolete before delivery and identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) between them.

Core Idea
When the price (fulūs) becomes worthless before delivery, Abu Hanifa voids the sale; the Sahibayn preserve it by substituting the value at contract or cessation date.

In this passage, we examine a well-known intra-Hanafi dispute about the fate of a sale when the currency (fulūs) ceases to be current before the buyer takes possession. This case mirrors an earlier dispute about mixed dirhams. Understanding the three positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani reveals how the Hanafi school balanced contractual stability with the integrity of the price.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the point of disagreement
The act is a sale where the price is fulūs that are current at the time of contract but become obsolete before delivery. The disagreement is whether the sale remains valid.
2
State the three positions
Abu Hanifa: sale void. Abu Yusuf: sale valid, pay value at contract date. Muhammad: sale valid, pay value at date of obsolescence.
3
Note the common ground and the divergence
All agree the buyer must compensate; the divergence is on the valuation date and whether the contract survives.
4
Recognize the analogy to the previous case
The passage says it is analogous to the dispute about mixed dirhams. The same rationales apply.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the seller can reclaim the goods; under the Sahibayn, the sale stands and the buyer owes a monetary obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا باع بالفلوس النافقة ثم كسدت بطل البيع عند ابي حنيفة خلافا لهما وهو نظير الاختلاف الذي بيناه

تشريح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی نے کوئی چیز رائج الوقت فلوس کے عوض فروخت کی پھر ان پر قبضہ کرنے سے پہلے ان کا چلن بند ہو گیا تو حضرت امام اعظم کے نزدیک یہ بیچ باطل ہو جائے گی اور صاحبین کے نزدیک بیع باطل نہ ہوگی بلکہ مشتری پر فلوس کا سدہ کی قیمت واجب ہوگی امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک یوم بیع کی قیمت واجب ہوگی اور امام محمد کے نزدیک یوم انقطاع کی قیمت واجب ہوگی اس اختلاف کی تفصیل مع الدائل گذشته مسئله اذا اشترى بها سلعة فكسدت کے تحت گذر چکی ہے ملاحظہ فرما لیا جائے۔
And if he sold with current fulūs and then they became obsolete, the sale is void according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And it is analogous to the disagreement we have explained.

Explanation: The issue is that if someone sold an item in exchange for current fulūs, then before taking possession [of the fulūs] their circulation ceased, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa this sale becomes void. According to the two, the sale does not become void; rather the buyer owes the value (price) of the fulūs. According to Imam Abu Yusuf, the value on the day of sale is owed; according to Imam Muhammad, the value on the day of cessation is owed. The detail of this disagreement with proofs has passed under the previous issue 'when one buys goods with them and they become obsolete' – please refer to it.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute parallel to the earlier one about mixed dirhams. When fulūs are current at the time of sale but become obsolete before the buyer takes possession, Imam Abu Hanifa rules the sale is void (bātil) because the price (fulūs) loses its thaman character. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold the sale valid, but differ on the valuation date: Abu Yusuf takes the day of sale, Muhammad takes the day of cessation (of circulation). The Urdu commentator notes that this is analogous to the previous discussion under 'If one buys goods with them and they become obsolete', and asks the reader to refer back for the proofs.

Key Terms
صاحبین (Sāḥibayn): The two companions: Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.
فلوس (fulūs): Coins that serve as currency; plural of fals. In this context, they are current (nāfiqa) at the time of sale but may become obsolete (kāsida).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) between the view of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding fulūs becoming obsolete before delivery?
A) Under Abu Hanifa the buyer must pay the value on the day of the contract; under the Sahibayn the sale is void.
B) Under Abu Hanifa the sale is void; under the Sahibayn the sale is valid and the buyer must pay the value of the fulūs.
C) Under all three imams the sale is void but the buyer pays compensation.
D) Under all three imams the sale is valid but the valuation date differs.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when fulūs become obsolete before delivery, the sale is void according to Abu Hanifa but valid according to the Sahibayn, with a further difference on the valuation date.

When Specification of Fulus Is Required in a Sale
States ruling on selling for fulus and when specification is required

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on selling goods for fulus and identify when specification of the fulus is required under Hanafi law.

Core Idea
Current fulus are like money and need not be specified; non-current fulus are goods and must be specified.

In this lesson, we examine a passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah that clarifies the permissibility of selling goods in exchange for fulus (copper coins). The ruling hinges on whether the fulus are currently in circulation as currency or have been withdrawn. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying Hanafi sales law to transactions involving non-standard currencies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of fulus
At the time of the contract, determine whether the fulus are current (ra'ij) or non-current (circulation ceased). This determines their legal nature.
Current fulus are treated as thaman (price) by custom; non-current fulus are goods (sama').
2
Apply the rules for current fulus
If the fulus are current, they are like dirhams and dinars: no specification is required, and specifying them does not make them determinate in the contract.
Example: buying a thing for ten paise – the paise need not be specified; the buyer simply pays ten paise.
3
Apply the rules for non-current fulus
If the fulus are not current, they are goods. In a sale of goods, specification is necessary for validity. Therefore, the fulus must be specifically identified.
Without specification, the sale is not valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصرف
اشرف الہدایہ جلد - تم
یہ ہے کہ فلوس کے عوض بیع جائز ہے کیونکہ فلوس ایسا مال نہیں جو مقدار اور وصف کے اعتبار سے معلوم ہو جاتے ہیں اور جو مال معلوم المقدار اور معلوم الوصف ہو اس کے عوض بیج جائز ہوتی ہے اس لئے فلوس کے عوض بیع جائز ہو جائے گی اب وہ فلوس عقد کے وقت یا تو رائج ہوں گے یا ان کا رواج ٹھپ ہو چکا ہوگا۔ اگر وہ رائج ہیں تو دراہم ودنا نیر کی طرح بیچ میں ان کا متعین کرنا ضروری نہیں ہے بلکہ وہ متعین کرنے سے بھی متعین نہ ہوں گے مثلاً : دس پیسے کی کوئی چیز خریدی تو اس چیز کا معین کرنا واجب ہے مگر پیسے معین نہ ہوں گے بلکہ پیسوں میں دس پیسے دیدے کیونکہ لوگوں کے اتفاق کر لینے سے فلوس شمن ہو گئے ہیں اور شمن متعین کرنے سے متعین نہیں ہوتا اس لئے یہ فلوس بھی متعین نہ ہوں گے اور اگر وہ فلوس رائج نہ ہوں بلکہ ان کا چلن بند ہو چکا ہو تو بیع میں ان کا معین کرنا ضروری ہے یعنی اس صورت میں ان کی بیچ اس وقت جائز ہوگی جبکہ ان کو متعین کر دیا گیا ہو کیونکہ اب یہ فلوس، سامان ہیں اور سامان کی بیع میں سامان کو متعین کرنا ضروری ہوتا ہے اس لیے ان کو متعین کرنا بھی ضروری ہوگا۔
Book of Currency Exchange. Ashraf al-Hidaya Volume - Think. This is that selling in exchange for fulus is permissible because fulus are not such property that can be known by measure and description, and that property which is known in measure and description, selling in exchange for it is permissible. Therefore selling in exchange for fulus becomes permissible. Now those fulus at the time of the contract are either current or their currency has stopped. If they are current, then like dirhams and dinars, it is not necessary to specify them in the contract; rather they are not specified even by specifying. For example: if someone buys a thing for ten paise, then specifying that thing is obligatory but the paise are not specified; rather he gives ten paise in payment because by the agreement of the people fulus have become thaman (price), and thaman is not specified by specifying, therefore these fulus also will not be specified. And if those fulus are not current but their circulation has ceased, then specifying them in the sale is necessary, i.e. in that case their sale is permissible only when they have been specified, because now these fulus are goods (sama') and in the sale of goods, specifying the goods is necessary, therefore specifying them also will be necessary.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah explains the ruling on selling goods in exchange for fulus (copper coins used as currency). It states that selling for fulus is permissible. The key factor is whether the fulus are currently circulating as currency (ra'ij) or have been withdrawn from circulation. If they are current, they are treated like gold and silver coins (dirhams and dinars) — they are considered thaman (price) by custom, and therefore do not need to be specified in the contract. Specifying them does not make them determinate; the buyer simply pays the quantity agreed upon. If the fulus are no longer current, they are treated as goods (sama') and must be specifically identified in the sale. The ruling is based on the nature of fulus: when current, they function as money; when not, they are ordinary commodities. This is a straightforward ruling with no ikhtilaf mentioned.

Key Terms
فلوس (fulus): Copper coins used as currency; in this text, they are either current (ra'ij) or withdrawn from circulation, affecting their legal status in sales.
شمن (thaman): Price or monetary consideration in a sale; in this text, fulus become thaman by mutual agreement/custom and therefore need not be specified.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on selling goods for fulus that are currently in circulation?
A) It is permissible, and the fulus must be specified in the contract.
B) It is permissible, and the fulus need not be specified; they are like dirhams and dinars.
C) It is not permissible because fulus are not valid currency.
D) It is permissible only if the fulus are specified in writing.

Key Takeaway
Selling for fulus is permissible, but specification is required only when the fulus are no longer in circulation; current fulus are treated as thaman by custom.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Sale with Demonetized Fulus Before Possession
Intra-Hanafi dispute on validity of sale when fulus become demonetized before possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three Hanafi positions (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) on the validity of a sale when fulus become demonetized before possession, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
When fulus become demonetized before possession, Abu Hanifa voids the sale; the Sahibayn uphold it with compensation.

This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of currency demonetization on a sale. Understanding the three positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad is essential for applying Hanafi law in markets where currency reforms occur. The disagreement hinges on whether fulus retain their status as thaman (price) after they cease to be current.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed act
The act is a sale where the price is designated in fulus (current currency) that become demonetized before the buyer takes possession.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa rules the sale void (batil) because the fulus lose their thaman status, rendering the consideration invalid.
The sale is nullified ab initio.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and its variants
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold the sale valid but require the buyer to pay the value of the fulus. Abu Yusuf fixes value at the day of sale; Muhammad fixes it at the day of demonetization.
The sale remains valid, but the price obligation transforms into a value obligation.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the seller must return the goods and the buyer returns the fulus (if any). Under the Sahibayn, the buyer keeps the goods and pays the equivalent value.
5
Note the absence of tarjih in the text
The passage does not specify which position is mufta bihi, but later Hanafi practice often adopts the Sahibayn's view for practicality.
The author refers to a past case for proofs; this is an expansion probe.

رائج فلوس کے عوض بیع کی پھر وہ کساد بازاری کا شکار ہو گئے تو بیع باطل ہے

واذا باع بالفلوس النافقة ثم كسدت بطل البيع عند ابي حنيفة خلافا لهما وهو نظير الاختلاف الذي بيناه ترجمہ اور اگر رائج الوقت فلوس کے عوض کوئی چیز فروخت کی پھر ان کا چلن بند ہو گیا تو ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک بیع باطل ہو جائے گی صاحبین کا اختلاف ہے اور یہ اس اختلاف کی نظیر ہے جس کو ہم نے بیان کیا ہے۔

Then if the sale was for current fulus and they became demonetized, the sale is invalid. And if he sold for current fulus then they became demonetized, the sale is void according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two (Sahibayn), and it is similar to the difference which we mentioned. Translation: And if he sold a thing in exchange for current fulus, then their circulation stopped, then according to Abu Hanifa the sale becomes void; the Sahibayn differ, and this is similar to the difference we have stated. Elaboration of the issue: If someone sold a thing in exchange for current fulus, then before taking possession their circulation stopped, then according to Imam A'zam this sale will become void, and according to the Sahibayn the sale will not be void; rather the buyer must pay the equivalent value of the fulus. Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a sale where the fulus used as consideration become demonetized before the buyer takes possession. Abu Hanifa rules that the sale becomes void (batil) because the fulus lose their status as thaman (price) and the consideration becomes unspecified. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the sale remains valid but the buyer must pay the value of the fulus instead. Between the two, Abu Yusuf says the value is determined at the time of the sale, while Muhammad says it is at the time of demonetization. The text notes that this dispute is analogous to a previous case (involving buying goods with fulus that become demonetized), and refers back to that earlier discussion for detailed proofs. This passage thus records the ikhtilaf without repeating the full evidence, emphasizing that the ruling depends on whether fulus are considered pure thaman or a commodity with secondary thaman status.

Key Terms
كسدت (kasadat): Became demonetized; fulus whose circulation as currency has stopped.
صاحبين (Sahibayn): The two disciples of Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Key Takeaway
In an intra-Hanafi dispute on fulus demonetization, the key is whether thaman status endures after kasād: Abu Hanifa voids the sale, while the two Sahibayn uphold it with compensation.

Repayment of a Loan of Fulus That Become Demonetized
Intra-Hanafi dispute on repayment of loan in fulus that become demonetized

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on repayment of a loan of fulus that become demonetized and explain the underlying reasoning (talil) of each imam.

Core Idea
When fulus become demonetized, Abu Hanifa requires return of the same number; the Sahibayn require payment of value.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the repayment of a loan of fulus (copper coins) that have been demonetized. Understanding this dispute deepens your grasp of how the Hanafi school treats fulus — as mithliyyat (fungibles) or as thaman (currency) — and how that classification affects debt repayment. The issue is not merely historical; similar questions arise today with demonetized currency or cryptocurrency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
What is the specific scenario? A person borrows fulus that are current (nafiqa), then they become demonetized (kasada) before repayment. The question is: what must the borrower return?
The passage assumes the fulus are mithliyyat (fungibles).
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling and talil
Abu Hanifa rules that the borrower must return the like (mithl) — the same number of fulus. His reasoning: a loan (qard) is like an 'ariyah (loan for use) in meaning; the obligation is to return the specific thing in meaning, not necessarily the actual physical item. The thaman (currency) status is an incidental attribute; fulus are primarily mithliyyat.
If the like were not treated as the actual, exchanging fulus for fulus with deferment would lead to riba.
3
State the Sahibayn's ruling and talil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule that the borrower must pay the value (qīmah) of the fulus. Their reasoning: when demonetization occurs, the attribute of thaman is voided, making it impossible to return the fulus as they were received (i.e., as currency). Hence, the value must be paid, analogous to a loan of a mithli that becomes unavailable.
The Sahibayn differ on the valuation date: Abu Yusuf says value at possession, Muhammad says value at demonetization.
4
Note the additional split between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
Abu Yusuf takes the value at the time of possession (day of contract), while Muhammad takes the value at the time of demonetization. This affects the amount the borrower must pay.
The text comments that Muhammad's view is fairer to both sides (anẓar lil-jānibayn) and Abu Yusuf's is easier (aysar).
5
Reflect on the tarjih and modern relevance
The passage does not explicitly choose a muftā bihi, but notes the relative strengths. Later Hanafi practice often follows the Sahibayn for practicality. Consider how this applies to modern cases like demonetized currency or cryptocurrency de-pegging.
The dispute is based on whether fulus are considered mithli or thaman; this affects many transactions.
