No Liability for Qadi or Amin When Price Lost After Sale of Mustahaqq Slave
Ruling with proof on immunity of qadi/amin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the liability of a qadi or amin for lost sale proceeds and explain the istihsan rationale based on public interest.

Core Idea
Public interest exempts the qadi and amin from liability to ensure they accept the trust.

When a qadi or his appointed trustee sells a debtor's slave to pay creditors, and the price is lost while the slave turns out to belong to another, is anyone liable? This passage from Al-Hidayah provides a clear ruling: neither the qadi nor the amin is liable. The buyer recovers from the creditors instead. The proof is a powerful example of istihsan based on public welfare.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and parties
Recognise that the qadi or his amin sells a slave belonging to a debtor (or deceased) for the benefit of creditors. The sale is initiated at the creditors' request.
The amin is a trustee appointed by the qadi.
2
State the ruling on liability
The ruling is that neither the qadi nor the amin is liable (no daman) for the price if it is lost after the sale, even if the slave is later found to belong to another (mustahaqq).
This is absolute immunity for accidental loss.
3
Identify the proof (dalil)
The proof is a rational argument (istihsan) based on public interest (maslaha): imposing liability would deter people from accepting the position of qadi or amin, leading to loss of people's rights.
The text explicitly says: 'so that people do not refrain from accepting this trust, and thus rights are lost.'
4
Understand the analogy to mahjur alayh
The buyer recovers the price from the creditors (not the qadi/amin) because the sale was for their benefit. This is analogous to a sale by an agent who is under legal interdiction (minor or unauthorised slave), where the buyer recovers from the principal.
The mahjur alayh cannot be held liable, so recourse goes to the one for whom the sale was made.
5
Apply the ruling to modern contexts
Consider how this ruling protects court-appointed receivers or trustees in bankruptcy proceedings from personal liability for accidental losses, ensuring that qualified persons accept such appointments.
The principle remains relevant in contemporary Islamic finance and judicial administration.

He said: And when the qadi or his amin sells a slave for the creditors, takes the money, then it is lost, and the slave is found to be owned by another (mustahaqq), he does not guarantee, because the amin of the qadi stands in the place of the qadi, and the qadi stands in the place of the imam, and none of them is liable for guarantee, so that people do not refrain from accepting this trust, and thus rights are lost. And the buyer recovers from the creditors, because the sale is for them, so he recovers from them when recovery from the contracting party is impossible, as when the contracting party is under interdiction (mahjur). For this reason, the sale is made at their request. Translation: And if the qadi or his amin sold a slave for the creditors and took the money, then the money was lost and the slave was found to be owned by another, then no one is a guarantor. Because the amin of the qadi is the deputy of the qadi, and the qadi is the deputy of the Imam of the Muslims, and no one among them incurs liability. So that people do not refuse to accept this trust, and thus rights are lost. And the buyer takes back his price from the creditors, because the sale is for them, so he recovers from them when recovery from the contracting party is impossible, just as when the contracting party is under interdiction; and for this reason, the sale is made at their request. Explanation: The case is that if the qadi or his amin sells the deceased's slave to pay the debts of the creditors, then the qadi or amin takes possession of the price, but the price is lost while in their possession, and the slave is found to be owned by another, i.e., someone proves ownership and takes the slave from the buyer. Then the contracting party, i.e., the qadi or his amin, is not liable for that price. Because the amin of the qadi is the deputy of the qadi, and the qadi is the deputy of the Imam of the Muslims. And the Imam, the qadi, and the amin — none of them are guarantors. Because if they were made guarantors, no one would accept this great office. And when no one accepts this trust (office), people's rights would be lost, whereas loss of rights is prohibited. Therefore, none of them is a guarantor. And the buyer takes back his price from the deceased's creditors, because the slave was sold for these creditors. This is why the qadi or his amin sells the deceased's slave at the request of the creditors. In any case, when the sale is for the creditors, and it becomes impossible to recover from the contracting party (the qadi or amin), then the price is taken back from those creditors, just as if the contracting party is under interdiction, i.e., a minor or an unauthorised slave, for example, someone appoints a discerning minor or an unauthorised slave as agent to sell his slave, and he sells the slave and takes the price, then the price is lost and the slave is found to be owned by another. Then the buyer cannot recover from the contracting party (the minor/slave), but recovers from their principal. Because this sale is for the principal. Similarly here, he recovers from the creditors, because in this case the sale is indeed for the creditors.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the liability of a qadi (judge) or his amin (trustee) when selling a debtor's slave to satisfy creditors. The ruling states that if the slave is later found to be owned by another (mustahaqq) and the sale price is lost, neither the qadi nor the amin is liable. The proof is based on maslaha (public interest): imposing liability would deter qualified people from accepting these positions, causing rights to be lost. The text compares this to the case where the contracting party is under interdiction (mahjur), where the buyer recovers from the principal. Here, since the sale is for the benefit of the creditors, the buyer recovers directly from them. The commentary elaborates with examples of minors and unauthorised slaves acting as agents.

Key Terms
أمين (amīn): A trustee appointed by the qadi to handle sales and manage assets
مستحق (mustaḥaqq): A slave that is later proven to belong to someone other than the seller, so the buyer must return it to the true owner
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability to compensate for loss or damage
محجور عليه (maḥjūr ʿalayhi): A person under legal interdiction, such as a minor or an unauthorised slave, who cannot be held liable for contractual losses

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a qadi sells a debtor's slave for creditors and the price is lost while the slave turns out to be owned by another, from whom does the buyer recover the price?
A) The qadi personally
B) The amin personally
C) The creditors
D) The debtor's estate

Key Takeaway
The qadi and amin are not liable for accidental loss of sale proceeds; the buyer recovers from the creditors because the sale was for their benefit, based on istihsan to preserve public welfare.

Buyer's Recourse When Qadi Orders Wasi to Sell
Ruling on buyer's recourse with qiyas and ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on buyer's recourse when a qadi orders a wasi to sell a deceased's property, explain the qiyas analogy, and identify the dispute among mashayikh regarding the creditor's compensation.

Core Idea
The wasi's contract binds the estate; the buyer recovers from the wasi, who then recovers from creditors.

This passage addresses a scenario where a qadi orders a wasi (executor) to sell a deceased debtor's slave to pay creditors. If the slave is found to be owned by another (mustahaqq) or dies before delivery (qabl al-qabd), and the sale price is lost, who bears the loss? The Hanafi ruling clarifies that the buyer recovers from the wasi, not the creditors directly, based on the principle that the wasi acts as the deceased's representative. The text then discusses the wasi's own recourse and a difference of opinion among the mashayikh regarding a creditor's ability to recover compensation paid to the wasi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
The qadi orders the wasi to sell the deceased's slave for the creditors. Two things can happen: the slave is taken by a rightful owner (mustahaqq) or dies before the buyer takes possession (qabl al-qabd), and the sale price is lost.
2
Determine the Buyer's Recourse
The text states unequivocally that the buyer recovers the lost price from the wasi. This is because the wasi is the direct contracting party representing the deceased.
The buyer does not recover from the creditors or the estate directly.
3
Understand the Qiyas Analogy
The reasoning is analogical: the wasi's position is like the deceased himself selling. 'فصار كما اذا باعه بنفسه' – it becomes as if the deceased sold it himself. So just as the buyer would recover from the deceased if he were alive, here he recovers from the wasi as the deceased's representative.
This is a qiyas (analogy) and the proof cited in the text.
4
Trace the Wasi's Own Recourse
After compensating the buyer, the wasi recovers from the creditors because he acted for their benefit. The creditors ultimately bear the loss as they were the beneficiaries of the sale.
If some property of the deceased appears later, the creditors recover their debts from it.
5
Examine the Ikhtilaf among Mashayikh
The mashayikh differ on whether a creditor who paid compensation (ta'awun) to the wasi can recover that amount from the deceased's estate. Some say yes, because it was incurred for the deceased's affairs; others say no, because the creditor is the beneficiary of the sale.
The text presents both views without settling the issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان امر القاضى الوصى ببيعه للغرماء ثم استحق اومات قبل القبض وضاع المال رجع المشترى على الوصى لانه عاقد نيابةً عن الميت وان كان باقامة القاضى عنه فصار كما اذا باعه بنفسه قال و يرجع الوصى على الغرماء لانه عامل لهم وان ظهر للميت مال يرجع الغريم فيه بدينه قالوا ويجوز ان يقال يرجع بالمائة التى غرمها أيضاً لانه لحقه فى امر الميت والوارث اذا بيع بمنزلة الغريم لانه اذا لم يكن في التركة دين كان العاقد عاملاً له.
ترجمہ اور اگر قاضی نے (میت قرضدار ) کے وصی کو حکم دیا کہ میت کا غلام اس کے قرض خواہوں کے واسطے فروخت کرے۔ پھر وہ غلام مستحق ہو گیا یا مشتری کے قبضہ سے پہلے مر گیا اور ثمن ضائع ہو گیا تو مشتری اپنا ثمن وصی سے واپس لے گا۔ اس لئے کہ وصی میت کی طرف سے نائب ہو کر عاقد ہے اگر چہ میت کی طرف سے قاضی نے اس کو کھڑا کیا ہے تو ایسا ہو گیا جیسے میت نے خود فروخت کیا اور وصی قرض خواہوں سے واپس لے گا۔ کیونکہ اس نے انہیں کے واسطے عمل کیا ہے اور اگر میت کے واسطے کوئی مال ظاہر ہوا تو قرض خواہ اس میں سے اپنا قرضہ وصول کر لے گا۔ مشائخ نے فرمایا کہ جائز ہے یہ کہا جائے کہ قرض خواہ وہ سو درہم بھی واپس لے گا جو اس نے وصی یا مشتری کو تاوان دیئے ہیں۔ اس لئے کہ یہ بھی اس کو میت کے معاملہ میں لاحق ہوئے ہیں۔ اور وارث کے لئے اگر ترکہ کا غلام فروخت کیا گیا تو بمنزلہ قرض خواہ کے ہے۔ اس لئے کہ جب ترکہ میں قرضہ نہ ہو تو عاقد وارث کے واسطے کام کرنے والا ہوگا۔
تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر قاضی نے میت قرضدار کے وصی کو حکم دیا کہ میت کا غلام اس کے قرض خواہوں کے واسطے فروخت کرے پھر غلام مشتری کے قبضہ میں جا کر مستحق ہو گیا یا مشتری کے قبضہ سے پہلے وہ مر گیا اور ثمن وصی کے پاس سے ضائع ہو گیا تو مشتری وصی سے اپنا شمن واپس لے گا۔ کیونکہ وصی تو میت کی طرف سے نائب ہو کر عقد کرنے والا ہے۔ چنانچہ اگر میت نے اس کو وصی مقرر کیا تھا تب تو اس کا نائب ہونا ظاہر ہے اور اگر قاضی نے مقرر کیا ہے تو بھی وہ میت کا نائب ہے اس لئے کہ قاضی نے اس کو میت کی طرف سے نائب مقرر کیا ہے نہ کہ اپنی طرف سے بہر حال وصی میت کا نائب ہے۔ اور نائب کا عقد ایسا ہے جیسے منوب عنہ کا عقد ہوتا ہے۔ پس یہ الیسا ہو گیا جیسا کہ میت نے خود اپنی زندگی میں فروخت کیا ہو۔ اور اس صورت میں مشتری مولیٰ سے رجوع کرنے کا مجاز تھا تو یہاں اس کے قائم مقام یعنی وسی سے رجوع کرنے کا مجاز ہوگا۔ پھر وصی میت کے قرضخواہوں سے رجوع کرے گا۔ کیونکہ دوسی نے انہیں کے واسطے کام کیا ہے۔ اور اگر میت کے واسطے کوئی مال ظاہر ہوا تو قرض خواہ اس میں سے اپنا قرضہ وصول کرلے گا۔
اب سوال یہ ہے کہ قرضخواہ نے وصی کو جو تاوان دیا ہے مثلاً سو درہم وہ بھی میت کے مال سے لے سکتا ہے یا نہیں؟ بعض مشائخ نے فرمایا کہ اس کو بھی لے سکتا ہے اس لئے کہ یہ بھی اس کو میت ہی کے معاملہ میں دینے پڑے ہیں۔ لہذا اس تاوان یعنی سو درہم کو بھی میت کے مال سے وصول کر لے گا۔ اور بعض مشائخ نے کہا کہ اس کے لئے تاوان کی رقم میت کے مال سے لینے کا حق نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ قرض خواہ اس لئے ضامن ہوا ہے کہ عقد اسی کے لئے واقع ہوا ہے۔ پس جب عقد قرض خواہ کے لئے واقع ہوا تو اس کو اپنے علاوہ سے تاوان کی رقم واپس
And if the qadi orders the wasi (executor) to sell it (the slave) for the creditors, then it is found to be owned by another (mustahaqq) or dies before delivery (qabl al-qabd) and the money is lost, the buyer recovers from the wasi, because he is the contracting party as a representative (na'ib) of the deceased, even though the qadi appointed him on behalf of the deceased; thus it becomes as if he himself sold it. He said: And the wasi recovers from the creditors, because he acted for them. And if some property of the deceased appears, the creditor recovers his debt from it. They (the mashayikh) said: It is permissible to say that the creditor also recovers the hundred dirhams that he paid as compensation (ta'awun), because it occurred to him in the matter of the deceased. And the heir, when the sale is made, is like the creditor, because when there is no debt in the estate, the contracting party acts for him. Translation: And if the qadi ordered the wasi of the deceased debtor to sell the deceased's slave for his creditors, then the slave either becomes mustahaqq or dies before the buyer takes possession, and the price is lost, then the buyer takes back his price from the wasi. Because the wasi is the contracting party as a representative of the deceased, even though the qadi appointed him on behalf of the deceased. So it becomes as if the deceased himself sold it. And the wasi recovers from the creditors, because he acted for them. And if some property of the deceased appears, the creditor recovers his debt from it. The mashayikh said: It is permissible to say that the creditor also recovers the hundred dirhams that he paid as compensation to the wasi or buyer, because it occurred to him in the matter of the deceased. And the heir, when the estate's slave is sold, is like the creditor, because when there is no debt in the estate, the contracting party acts for him. Explanation: The case is that if the qadi ordered the wasi of the deceased debtor to sell the deceased's slave for his creditors, then the slave becomes mustahaqq after the buyer takes possession, or dies before the buyer takes possession, and the price is lost while in the wasi's possession, then the buyer takes back his price from the wasi. Because the wasi is the contracting party as a representative of the deceased. If the deceased appointed him as wasi, his being a representative is clear; and even if the qadi appointed him, he is still a representative of the deceased because the qadi appointed him on behalf of the deceased, not on his own behalf. In any case, the wasi is the representative of the deceased. And the contract of a representative is like the contract of the represented one. So it becomes as if the deceased himself sold it in his lifetime. And in that case, the buyer was entitled to recover from the master (the deceased); so here he is entitled to recover from his representative, the wasi. Then the wasi recovers from the deceased's creditors, because he acted for them. And if some property of the deceased appears, the creditor recovers his debt from it. Now the question is: Can the creditor also recover from the deceased's estate the compensation he paid to the wasi, e.g., a hundred dirhams? Some mashayikh said he can recover it, because this also occurred to him in the matter of the deceased. So he also recovers that compensation from the deceased's estate. And some mashayikh said he does not have the right to take the compensation amount from the deceased's estate, because the creditor became a guarantor only because the contract was made for him. So when the contract was for the creditor, he cannot recover the compensation from someone else.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on a scenario where a qadi orders a wasi (executor) to sell a deceased debtor's slave to pay creditors. If the slave turns out to be owned by another (mustahaqq) or dies before delivery, and the sale price is lost, the buyer can recover the price from the wasi. The reason is that the wasi acts as a representative of the deceased, whether appointed by the deceased or by the qadi. The wasi then recovers from the creditors because he acted for their benefit. The passage also discusses a difference of opinion among mashayikh: whether a creditor who paid compensation to the wasi (e.g., for a loss) can recover that amount from the deceased's estate. Some say yes, because it is an expense incurred for the deceased's affairs; others say no, because the creditor is the beneficiary of the sale. The text ends with a mention that an heir is like a creditor when the estate has no debts, meaning the sale is for the heir's benefit.

Key Terms
وصي (waṣī): An executor or administrator appointed to manage the deceased's estate, either by the deceased or by the qadi
تاوان (taʿāwun): Compensation paid by one party to another for a loss, here the amount paid by the creditor to the wasi or buyer
قبض (qabḍ): Taking physical or constructive possession of the sold item
مال (māl): The sale price (thaman) that is lost

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a qadi orders a wasi to sell a deceased's slave for creditors and the slave dies before delivery (qabl al-qabd) and the price is lost, from whom does the buyer recover the price?
A) The qadi
B) The creditors directly
C) The wasi
D) The deceased's estate

Key Takeaway
The buyer's recourse is against the wasi, not the creditors, because the wasi represents the deceased; the wasi then recovers from the creditors.
